<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome expansion of Arabis alpina linked with retrotransposition and reduced symmetric DNA methylation"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2015 1:2"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2015-02-02"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2015 Macmillan Publishers Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Despite evolutionary conserved mechanisms to silence transposable element activity, there are drastic differences in the abundance of transposable elements even among closely related plant species. We conducted a de novo assembly for the 375&#8197;Mb genome of the perennial model plant, Arabis alpina. Analysing this genome revealed long-lasting and recent transposable element activity predominately driven by Gypsy long terminal repeat retrotransposons, which extended the low-recombining pericentromeres and transformed large formerly euchromatic regions into repeat-rich pericentromeric regions. This reduced capacity for long terminal repeat retrotransposon silencing and removal in A. alpina co-occurs with unexpectedly low levels of DNA methylation. Most remarkably, the striking reduction of symmetrical CG and CHG methylation suggests weakened DNA methylation maintenance in A. alpina compared with Arabidopsis thaliana. Phylogenetic analyses indicate a highly dynamic evolution of some components of methylation maintenance machinery that might be related to the unique methylation in A. alpina. Closely related plant species can have drastically different abundances of transposable elements. Now it is shown that the genome of Arabis alpina has experienced a striking expansion of retrotransposons, probably shaped by reduced DNA methylation."/>

        <meta property="og:description" content="Closely related plant species can have drastically different abundances of transposable elements. Now it is shown that the genome of Arabis alpina has experienced a striking expansion of retrotransposons, probably shaped by reduced DNA methylation."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Despite evolutionary conserved mechanisms to silence transposable element activity, there are drastic differences in the abundance of transposable elements even among closely related plant species. We conducted a <i>de novo</i> assembly for the 375 Mb genome of the perennial model plant, <i>Arabis alpina</i>. Analysing this genome revealed long-lasting and recent transposable element activity predominately driven by <i>Gypsy</i> long terminal repeat retrotransposons, which extended the low-recombining pericentromeres and transformed large formerly euchromatic regions into repeat-rich pericentromeric regions. This reduced capacity for long terminal repeat retrotransposon silencing and removal in <i>A. alpina</i> co-occurs with unexpectedly low levels of DNA methylation. Most remarkably, the striking reduction of symmetrical CG and CHG methylation suggests weakened DNA methylation maintenance in <i>A. alpina</i> compared with <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. Phylogenetic analyses indicate a highly dynamic evolution of some components of methylation maintenance machinery that might be related to the unique methylation in <i>A. alpina</i>.</p></div></div></section>

                    <div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Whole-genome sequences of members of the Brassicaceae family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1136">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nature Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1139">10</a></sup> (including the plant model <i>A. thaliana</i>) are greatly expanding the scope for comparative genomics among closely related plant species (for example refs <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wu, H-J. et al. Insights into salt tolerance from the genome of Thellungiella salsuginea. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 12219–12224 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1145">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Haudry, A. et al. An atlas of over 90,000 conserved noncoding sequences provides insight into crucifer regulatory regions. Nature Genet. 45, 891–898 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1148">8</a>). Despite their close phylogenetic relationship, Brassicaceae species have repeatedly evolved many differences in important life history traits, including the capacity for self-fertilization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Tedder, A., Ansell, S. W., Lao, X., Vogel, J. C. &amp; Mable, B. K. Sporophytic self-incompatibility genes and mating system variation in Arabis alpina. Ann. Bot. 108, 699–713 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1152">11</a></sup>, senescence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Wingler, A., Stangberg, E. J., Saxena, T. &amp; Mistry, R. Interactions between temperature and sugars in the regulation of leaf senescence in the perennial herb Arabis alpina L. J. Integr. Plant Biol. 54, 595–605 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1157">12</a></sup> and annual or perennial flowering behavior<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wang, R. et al. PEP1 regulates perennial flowering in Arabis alpina. Nature 459, 423–427 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1161">13</a></sup>.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The reference accession of the perennial <i>A. alpina</i> Pajares was collected in the Cordillera Cantábrica mountains of Spain and was self-fertilized for six generations by single-seed descent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wang, R. et al. PEP1 regulates perennial flowering in Arabis alpina. Nature 459, 423–427 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1171">13</a></sup>. We generated a high-quality 309 Mb genome assembly from a mixture of 454 Illumina and Sanger BAC end sequences with scaffolds with an N50 of 788 kb and L50 of 160 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Tables 1 and 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>). By integrating comparative chromosome painting, we arranged more than 85% of the large scaffolds (&gt;50 kb) into eight pseudo-molecules representing the eight chromosomes of <i>A. alpina</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Figs 2–4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>). During a manual annotation jamboree the structural annotation of 514 selected genes was curated from a total of 30,729 genes and 278,110 repetitive elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Tables 3 and 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>). To resolve the phylogenetic placement of <i>A. alpina</i>, we calculated a whole genome-based phylogenetic consensus network for 10 Brassicaceae species with available reference assemblies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). In addition to its topology, neutral variation and chromosome rearrangements specific to the karyotype of <i>A. alpina</i> argue for <i>A. alpina</i> being a member of a separate lineage that diverged from the <i>Arabidopsis</i> lineage around 27 ± 16 Ma (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Figs 5–7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Phylogenetic reconstruction and karyotype evolution support a distinct phylogenetic placement of A. alpina."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Phylogenetic reconstruction and karyotype evolution support a distinct phylogenetic placement of <i>A. alpina</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201423/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2014.23/MediaObjects/41477_2015_Article_BFnplants201423_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2014.23/MediaObjects/41477_2015_Article_BFnplants201423_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="446" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Consensus network of the Brassicaceae phylogeny based on 1,787 single-copy clusters of orthologous genes. Its topology did not unambiguously place the <i>Arabis</i> species with Lineage II as proposed earlier. (Lineage I species (red); Lineage II species (blue); <i>Arabis</i> species (green)). <b>b</b>, Karyotype evolution at the base of Brassicaceae evolution. Reconstruction of the chromosome evolution from the Ancestral Crucifer Karyotype (ACK) to the <i>A. alpina</i> karyotype (KAA) suggested nine chromosomal rearrangements, which are different from the rearrangements that occurred in the evolution from the ACK to the Proto-Calepineae Karyotype (PCK), which is ancestral to Lineage II. <b>c</b>, Brassicaceae genome compositions. TEs, transposable elements</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201423/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Within current annotations of Brassicaceae genomes, genes and introns account for 58–81 Mb, whereas transposable element content is much more variable and accounts for up to 148 Mb in <i>A. alpina</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). By far the most abundant TE superfamily in <i>A. alpina</i> is the long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons superfamily <i>Ty3/Gypsy</i> (or <i>Gypsies</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Table 5</a>). An increasing number of plant genomes have pointed to recent bursts of LTR retrotransposon transposition as a common phenomenon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nature Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1289">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Sanmiguel, P. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Evidence that a recent increase in maize genome size was caused by the massive amplification of intergene retrotransposons. Ann. Bot. 82, 37–44 (1998)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1292">14</a></sup>. A hallmark of such recent transpositions is large amounts of young copies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Slotte, T. et al. The Capsella rubella genome and the genomic consequences of rapid mating system evolution. Nature Genet. 45, 831–835 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1296">9</a></sup> (&gt;95% sequence similarity). However, in <i>A. alpina</i> we found a large fraction of middle-aged transposable elements (85–95% sequence similarity)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Maumus, F. &amp; Quesneville, H. Ancestral repeats have shaped epigenome and genome composition for millions of years in Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature Commun. 5, 4104 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1303">15</a></sup>. The reduced amount of very young elements most likely indicates a recent reduction of <i>Gypsy</i> element activity as only small parts of this result from inefficiencies in short read assembly. Rather large parts of the non-assembled sequence relates to simple sequence repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Figs 8 and 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>). Intriguingly, only the <i>Gypsies</i> contribute to the high number of middle-aged transposable elements in <i>A. alpina</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). This suggests that <i>A. alpina Gypsy</i> elements proliferated over an extended period of time and that a large amount of these elements was retained and removed at only slow rates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Ma, J. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Rapid recent growth and divergence of rice nuclear genomes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 12404–12410 (2004)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1333">16</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome size variation and differences in transposable element content."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Genome size variation and differences in transposable element content.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201423/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2014.23/MediaObjects/41477_2015_Article_BFnplants201423_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2014.23/MediaObjects/41477_2015_Article_BFnplants201423_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="405" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, transposable element age spectra based on similarity between transposable element copies and consensus sequence. <i>A. alpina</i> shows a unique increase in middle-aged <i>Gypsy</i> elements. <b>b</b>, Fraction of genes and transposable elements marked with H3K4me3. The three largest superfamilies are shown separately. <b>c</b>, Age distribution of <i>Gypsies</i> inside and outside of genes separated by their different H3K4me3 markings. <b>d</b>, Expression of individual transposable element superfamilies in <i>A. alpina</i>. <b>e</b>, Fraction of expressed transposable elements with and without H3K4me3 markings. <b>f</b>, Size of the ten largest transposable element families in <i>A. alpina</i> along with their family-wide fraction of H3K4me3 marks within all three species.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201423/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>To analyse the degree to which these TEs may be epigenetically silenced, we assayed four distinct chromatin marks in <i>A. alpina, A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i>, including histone modifications H3K4me3, H3K27me3 and H3K27me1 assayed by ChIP-seq, and DNA methylation assayed by immunoprecipitation of methylated DNA coupled with high-throughput sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhang, X. et al. Genome-wide high-resolution mapping and functional analysis of DNA methylation in arabidopsis. Cell 126, 1189–1201 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1399">17</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>). H3K27me1 and DNA methylation mark epigenetically silenced TEs, whereas H3K27me3 is a repressive mark specifically associated with genes and H3K4me3 is associated with regions that are actively transcribed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Roudier, F. et al. Integrative epigenomic mapping defines four main chromatin states in Arabidopsis. EMBO J. 30, 1928–1938 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1406">18</a></sup>. As in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Roudier, F. et al. Integrative epigenomic mapping defines four main chromatin states in Arabidopsis. EMBO J. 30, 1928–1938 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1413">18</a></sup>, DNA methylation and H3K27me1 modification were mostly associated with TEs in <i>A. alpina</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i>, whereas H3K4me3 and H3K27me3 were preferentially associated with genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). In <i>A. alpina</i>, however, we found a three times larger proportion of TEs marked with H3K4me3 as in the other two species. <i>Gypsies</i> showed by far the largest fraction of elements marked with H3K4me3, whereas all other superfamilies did not show such a pronounced increase (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Figs 10, 11</a>).</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Even though <i>Gypsies</i> within genes were more likely to be marked with H3K4me3 and were consistently older in all three species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>), <i>A. alpina</i> showed only a slightly increased fraction (7.8%) of <i>Gypsies</i> in genes compared with <i>A. thaliana</i> (5.7%) and much less than <i>A. lyrata</i> (15.1%), implying that elevated levels of H3K4me3 marking among <i>Gypsies</i> were not dominated by epigenetic states of genes. Moreover, H3K4me3-marked <i>Gypsy</i> elements outside of genes were drastically younger than those without this mark in <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> but not in <i>A. alpina</i>, suggesting that many of these elements might have retained the ability to be transcribed over time. In fact, when analysing the 1.5% of the RNA-sequencing (seq) reads that were not assigned to genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Slotte, T. et al. The Capsella rubella genome and the genomic consequences of rapid mating system evolution. Nature Genet. 45, 831–835 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1480">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Hollister, J. D., Smith, L. M., Guo, Y-L., Ott, F., Weigel, D. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Transposable elements and small RNAs contribute to gene expression divergence between Arabidopsis thaliana and Arabidopsis lyrata. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 2322–2327 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1483">19</a></sup>, we found that the <i>Gypsy</i> superfamily was more expressed than any of the other superfamilies in <i>A. alpina</i>, in contrast to <i>A. thaliana</i> in which Copia showed the highest fraction of RNA-seq reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig2">Fig. 2d</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Fig. 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>). Moreover, transposable elements with the H3K4me3 mark were significantly enriched for expressed TEs compared with TEs without this mark across all large superfamilies, even though this effect was less pronounced for <i>Gypsies</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig2">Fig. 2e</a>).</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Two <i>Gypsy</i> families, <i>ATGPI</i> and <i>ATLANTYS2</i>, accounted for more than a fifth of all <i>Gypsy</i> elements in <i>A. alpina</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">2f and Supplementary Note</a>). These two families showed an even more drastic increase in elements marked with H3K4me3, which was not apparent in any of the other <i>A. alpina</i> TE families, and together with their increased copy number and age distribution this suggests that the observed burst of transposition was mostly driven by this small group of TEs.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>In <i>A. alpina, A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i>, TE density increases towards the centromeres<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1547">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nature Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1550">2</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Figs 13 and 14</a>). Typically these repeat-rich regions overlap with heterochromatic pericentromeres. Here we defined pericentromeres as regions with high amounts of H3K27me1 surrounding the centromeres (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Table 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>). Pericentromeres in <i>A. alpina</i> were drastically larger (average length: 14.9 Mb in <i>A. alpina</i>, 3.9 Mb in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1575">1</a></sup>, 10.3 Mb in <i>A. lyrata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nature Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1582">2</a></sup>) and included many more genes than the other two species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). <i>Gypsy</i> elements are significantly enriched among the repeats in pericentromeres (<i>U</i>-test, <i>P</i>-value &lt; 2e–16) and account for many of the size differences of pericentromeres (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). In <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> H3K4me3 markings are strongly correlated with gene density throughout the chromosomes. In <i>A. alpina,</i> however, this correlation was weak and even entirely missing in pericentromeres, where H3K4me3 was slightly correlated with <i>Gypsy</i> element density instead, suggesting that <i>Gypsies</i> are epigenetically active even in the heterochromatic pericentromere in <i>A. alpina</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Figs 15–17</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Differences in the distribution of genes, transposable elements and chromatin marks between A. thaliana, A. lyrata and A. alpina."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Differences in the distribution of genes, transposable elements and chromatin marks between <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>A. lyrata</i> and <i>A. alpina</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201423/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2014.23/MediaObjects/41477_2015_Article_BFnplants201423_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2014.23/MediaObjects/41477_2015_Article_BFnplants201423_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="736" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Gene, transposable element and histone mark density, along orthologous chromosomes (missing sequence marked in grey). <b>b</b>, Genomic fraction and gene space in chromosome arms and pericentromeres. <b>c</b>, Genome coverage of the three largest transposable element superfamilies. <b>d</b>, Comparison of <i>A. alpina</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i> chromosome 2 sharing the same ancestral centromere. Grey lines connect single-gene orthologues. Orthologues that reside in pericentromeric regions in <i>A. alpina</i>, but are outside these regions in <i>A. lyrata</i>, are indicated by dark grey lines. Locations of solo-LTRs indicated by grey crosses. (Gene and transposable element densities as in <b>a</b>, crossover frequency (red), pericentromeres (dark brown)).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201423/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Genome size differences between Brassicaceae species have previously been attributed to pericentromere expansion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Yang, R. et al. The reference genome of the halophytic plant Eutrema salsugineum. Front. Plant Sci. 4, 46 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1687">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hall, A. E., Kettler, G. C. &amp; Preuss, D. Dynamic evolution at pericentromeres. Genome Res. 16, 355–364 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1690">20</a></sup>, but the causes and functional consequences have remained unclear. Centromeres in many species suppress crossover recombination during meiosis, a phenomenon that usually extends into heterochromatic regions near the centromere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wijnker, E. et al. The genomic landscape of meiotic crossovers and gene conversions in Arabidopsis thaliana. eLife 2, e01426 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1694">21</a></sup>. Crossover frequencies along seven investigated chromosomes of <i>A. alpina</i> revealed for each chromosome a region with suppressed crossovers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Tables 7, 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>). These regions co-localize with large parts of the pericentromeres implying that the extent of non-recombining DNA in <i>A. alpina</i> is greatly increased compared with <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>A. lyrata</i>.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Earlier analyses reported differences in gene content in pericentromeres of Brassicaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Hall, A. E., Kettler, G. C. &amp; Preuss, D. Dynamic evolution at pericentromeres. Genome Res. 16, 355–364 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1720">20</a></sup>, but were complicated by the lack of whole-genome sequences. Reconstruction of ancestral chromosomal rearrangements of <i>A. alpina</i> revealed a single homologous paleocentromere (chromosome 2) with <i>A. lyrata</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu, T. T. et al. The Arabidopsis lyrata genome sequence and the basis of rapid genome size change. Nature Genet. 43, 476–481 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1729">2</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Figs 2 and 3</a>). The assembly of the long arm of chromosome 2 shows a clear transition between gene- and repeat-rich regions in both species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a>). Near the transition zone, there are 207 single-copy orthologues that reside in the repeat-dense regions in <i>A. alpina</i>, but outside the pericentromere in gene-rich regions in <i>A. lyrata.</i> Comparing two sparse genetic maps of these species suggested that the repeat-rich region in <i>A. alpina</i> shows more strongly suppressed recombination than the orthologous regions in <i>A. lyrata</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Kuittinen, H. et al. Comparing the linkage maps of the close relatives Arabidopsis lyrata and A. thaliana. Genetics 168, 1575–1584 (2004)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1759">22</a></sup>. This implies that upon expansion of the repeat-rich pericentromeric regions in <i>A. alpina</i>, genes in formerly gene-rich regions became incorporated into the pericentromere, with the consequence that large clusters of genes experience very little meiotic recombination in <i>A. alpina</i>. Although we cannot fully exclude the possibility of accelerated loss of transposable elements and pericentromere shrinkage in <i>A. lyrata</i>, we found no evidence for large numbers of solo LTRs that would indicate continuing loss through unequal homologous deletions in this particular genomic region (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>).</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Increased transposable element activity and gain of H3K4me3 has been linked to reduction in DNA methylation at transposable elements in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Zhang, X., Bernatavichute, Y. V., Cokus, S., Pellegrini, M. &amp; Jacobsen, S. E. Genome-wide analysis of mono-, di- and trimethylation of histone H3 lysine 4 in Arabidopsis thaliana. Genome Biol. 10, R62 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1783">23</a></sup>. To further examine DNA methylation in <i>A. alpina</i>, we performed whole-genome bisulphite sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Cokus, S. J. et al. Shotgun bisulphite sequencing of the Arabidopsis genome reveals DNA methylation patterning. Nature 452, 215–219 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1790">24</a></sup> using leaf material and compared it with analogous data previously generated for <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Becker, C. et al. Spontaneous epigenetic variation in the Arabidopsis thaliana methylome. Nature 480, 245–249 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1796">25</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Note</a>). Though this revealed similar amounts of methylated cytosines in <i>A. alpina</i> (19%) and <i>A. thaliana</i> (16%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Becker, C. et al. Spontaneous epigenetic variation in the Arabidopsis thaliana methylome. Nature 480, 245–249 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1810">25</a></sup> and similar methylation profiles along genes and transposable elements in both species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Cokus, S. J. et al. Shotgun bisulphite sequencing of the Arabidopsis genome reveals DNA methylation patterning. Nature 452, 215–219 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1814">24</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>), the position-wise frequency of CG methylation was strikingly different. Whereas most methylated CGs showed 80–100% methylation in <i>A. thaliana</i>, these levels tended to be much lower in <i>A. alpina</i> irrespective of sequence annotation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig4">Fig. 4a,b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>). In contrast, the distribution of methylation levels at CHG sites was only slightly shifted towards lower values in <i>A. alpina</i>, and was very similar for CHH sites. In <i>A. thaliana</i>, CG and CHG methylation typically occurs on both Cs of the opposite strands of these palindromes, indicative of methylation copying via the maintenance machinery during replication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Cokus, S. J. et al. Shotgun bisulphite sequencing of the Arabidopsis genome reveals DNA methylation patterning. Nature 452, 215–219 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1841">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Law, J. A. &amp; Jacobsen, S. E. Establishing, maintaining and modifying DNA methylation patterns in plants and animals. Nature Rev. Genet. 11, 204–220 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1844">26</a></sup>. Surprisingly, the two strands are essentially uncorrelated in their methylation levels at CG sites, and much more weakly correlated at CHG sites in <i>A. alpina</i> throughout the entire genome, suggesting that methylation maintenance is much less pervasive (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Species-specific differences in DNA methylation."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Species-specific differences in DNA methylation.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201423/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2014.23/MediaObjects/41477_2015_Article_BFnplants201423_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2014.23/MediaObjects/41477_2015_Article_BFnplants201423_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="544" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Position-wise DNA methylation frequencies. <b>b</b>, DNA methylation frequencies in <i>A. alpina</i> separated by genomic regions. <b>c</b>, Correlation of methylation frequency on Watson and Crick strand at symmetrical CG and CHG sites (Aa, <i>A. alpina</i>; At, <i>A. thaliana</i>). <b>d</b>, Gene family sizes of DNA methylation genes. <b>e</b>–<b>i</b>, Gene family phylogenies (Aa, <i>A. alpina</i>; Al, <i>A. lyrata</i>, At, <i>A. thaliana</i>; Br, <i>B. rapa</i>; Cp, <i>C. papaya</i>, Cr, <i>C. rubella</i>; Es, <i>E. salsugineum</i>, Sp, <i>S. parvula</i>; Lineage I species (red); Lineage II species (blue); <i>A. alpina</i> (green)). <b>j</b>, <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values for orthologous genes pairs between <i>A. alpina</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> (light blue) and <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> values of each methylation gene family (coloured dots).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants201423/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Given these fundamental differences, we suspected that the DNA methylation maintenance machinery might function differently in <i>A. alpina</i>. To explore this possibility, we examined the Brassicaceae genomes for intact homologues of the five major gene families involved in DNA methylation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Law, J. A. &amp; Jacobsen, S. E. Establishing, maintaining and modifying DNA methylation patterns in plants and animals. Nature Rev. Genet. 11, 204–220 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4708e1965">26</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig4">Fig. 4d</a>). At least one homologue of each family was found and showed expression in <i>A. alpina</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#MOESM147">Supplementary Table 9</a>). The phylogenies of <i>DDM1</i>, required for CG methylation maintenance, <i>CMT3</i>, required for CHG methylation maintenance and <i>DRM2</i>, involved in <i>de novo</i> methylation in all contexts, broadly recapitulated the family phylogeny (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig4">Fig. 4e–g</a>). In contrast, the homologues of <i>MET1</i> and <i>VIM1</i>, which in addition to <i>DDM1</i> are essential for CG methylation maintenance in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Law, J. A. &amp; Jacobsen, S. E. Establishing, maintaining and modifying DNA methylation patterns in plants and animals. Nature Rev. Genet. 11, 204–220 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4708e2006">26</a></sup>, clustered in a species- and lineage-specific manner (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig4">Fig. 4h,i</a>). This implies that all species outside the <i>Arabidopsis</i> lineage lacked clear orthologues for <i>MET1</i> and <i>VIM1</i> genes, which was also apparent from the lack of synteny of these genes with any of their homologues outside this lineage. Moreover, the ratio of mutation rates at nonsynonymous (N) and synonymous (S) sites <i>d</i><sub>N</sub>/<i>d</i><sub>S</sub> calculated for each gene family revealed values highly similar to a genome-wide background distribution, except for <i>MET1</i> family members with consistently enriched values, suggesting that less purifying selection pressure acts on <i>MET1</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants201423#Fig4">Fig. 4j</a>).</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Although <i>MET1</i> and <i>VIM1</i> homologues are present in <i>A. alpina</i>, it remains possible that the lineage-specific evolution of these genes might relate to the differences in CG methylation maintenance, as homologues of the main methylation genes are present in other species with strong differences in DNA methylation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Zemach, A., McDaniel, I. E., Silva, P. &amp; Zilberman, D. Genome-wide evolutionary analysis of eukaryotic DNA Methylation. Science 328, 916–919 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4708e2054">27</a></sup>. However, more complex changes in other methylation pathways might need to be considered to reveal the basis of DNA methylation differences between <i>A. alpina</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>. As the absence of symmetrical CG methylation levels did not correlate with an overall lower amount of at least partially methylated cytosines, <i>de novo</i> DNA methylation probably compensates for the lack of DNA methylation maintenance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Matzke, M., Kanno, T., Daxinger, L., Huettel, B. &amp; Matzke, A. J. M. RNA-mediated chromatin-based silencing in plants. Curr. Opin. Cell Biol. 21, 367–376 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4708e2068">28</a></sup> underlining the high importance of <i>de novo</i> DNA methylation in <i>A. alpina</i>.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Even though co-occurrence of expanded transposable element content and DNA methylation maintenance deficiency in <i>A. alpina</i> does not necessarily imply a causal relationship, it nevertheless remains an attractive possibility that apparent methylation deficiency might have contributed to the elevated numbers of <i>Gypsy</i> elements, possibly due to reduced silencing of specific transposable element families, as was shown for DNA methylation maintenance deficient mutants in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tsukahara, S., Kobayashi, A., Kawabe, A., Mathieu, O., Miura, A. &amp; Kakutani, T. Bursts of retrotransposition reproduced in Arabidopsis. Nature 461, 423–426 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4708e2089">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Mirouze, M. et al. Selective epigenetic control of retrotransposition in Arabidopsis. Nature 461, 427–430 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants201423#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4708e2092">30</a></sup>.</p></div><section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>All data from this study have been deposited at the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under BioProject PRJNA241291. The whole genome assembly of <i>A. alpina</i> has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession JNGA00000000. The version described in this manuscript is version JNGA01000000.</p></div></div></section>

L.C., M.C.A., B.S., S.B., L.C., J.L.M., M.C.B., N.B., T.P., L.D.L., I.M. and C.B.S. prepared samples for DNA and RNA sequencing. B.S. prepared samples and performed ChIP and MeDIP experiments. C.B. prepared samples and performed bisulphite experiments. N.W., M.C.A. and C.A-B. constructed genetic maps. T.M. and M.A.L. conducted FISH, chromosome painting and karyotype evolution analysis. K.J.V.N., E.M.W. and N.W. conducted <i>de novo</i> assembly of <i>A. alpina</i>. C.K. conducted <i>de novo</i> assembly of <i>A. montbretiana</i>. G.V.J., E.M.W., C.B.S. and M.Z. performed genome annotations of <i>A. alpina</i> together with all participants of the annotation jamboree held in 2012 in Paris, France. E.M.W. performed genome annotations of <i>A. montbretiana</i>. E.M.W., V.R. and C.C. conducted expression analyses. F.M. performed transposon annotations. E.M.W., F.M., M.P. and K.S. performed transposon analysis. E.M.W., C.C., R.C.M.T. and K.S. performed analysis of ChIP-seq and MeDIP-seq data. E.M.W., C.B., J.H. and K.S. performed BS-seq analysis. E.M.W., V.R. and K.S. conducted comparative genomic analyses. E.M.W. and K.S. wrote the paper with contributions from all authors.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nplants201423/email/correspondent/c1/new">George Coupland</a> or <a id="corresp-c2" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nplants201423/email/correspondent/c2/new">Korbinian Schneeberger</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The authors declare that no competing interests exist.</p>
